<question>



What do i need to buy for a level fine art art?



what equipment do i need to buy to prepare for fine art? thanks hettie



</question>



<answer from>



Voice
Member since: 17 August 2008
Total points: 174 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



Are you in college?  If so don't worry about it until the first day of class, because art classes cast a bundle.  Always have on hand a #2 pencil, but be prepared you may need a pencil set with the different shades. Ex. H, #2, #5, etc.  You could always e-mail your instructor and ask or ask at the book store.  Before you buy at the college book store check at the local arts and craft stores, sometimes they are a whole lot cheaper.  Consider a sketch book, but the instructor may want you to purchase a certain size or paper quality.  Good Luck You Will Do Fine!



Source(s):



Art student



</answer>



<answer from>



Jeff (weseye) Wesley
Member since: 22 January 2008
Total points: 1840 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



It would be helpful if you had some ability, talent and a desire to art. It is a hard row to hoe to become famous or successful as a fine artist. It can be done but there are no guarantees.

Good luck!
Keep Arting!
Jeff (weseye) Wesley



</answer>



<answer from>



Tine B
Member since: 17 August 2008
Total points: 150 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



if you're starting college then a range of pencils, oil pastels, paint brushes and soft pastels to start of with would be a good idea, go to an art shop and explain to the shopkeeper what your situation is and they should be able to help you out a bit, i wouldn't recommend buying a sketchbook just yet, lecturers often request a certain sized one for certain project,



Source(s):



fine art student



</answer>

Contributing In:

<answer>



Go to a craft store and get some thin but sturdy wire; shape them into wings and wrap some thin netting around them.  Then decorate the netting with some glitter or sequins.



</answer>

Member since: 15 August 2008

</answer>



<answer from>



Franklin V
Member since: 03 May 2007
Total points: 375 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



moderately thick wire, like from a clothes hangar.  bend them into the shape you want.  cover them with coloured tights/pantyhose.  put a line of glue around the edges and trim off the excess bit of nylons.  spray with aerosol adhesive and add just the tiniest bit of glitter before it dries.attach some elastic to loop around your arms so it will hang off your back.



</answer>



<answer from>



Tizzie
Member since: 18 November 2007
Total points: 3619 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



The very simplest way is to attach the fabric you are using to the centre back of the dress and then attach the ends of it to the wrists by means of elastic, in such a way that when the arms are raised the 'wings' are raised.  Hope this is understandable!



</answer>

<answer from>



Celestis
Member since: 22 July 2008
Total points: 394 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



What took me the longest to learn (and it's a matter of practice) is to draw what you "see"...sounds easy right? But it's not because we have all these preconcieved notions of how things look already in our minds. So we gravitate to draw according to that. For example...if you draw a face...you draw a sort of oval shape, two eyes on the top part, nose and mouth. But when you actually look at a person, really look, then you would see that the head is really a different shape, the eyes are more in the center and so on. 
Our teacher made us practice drawing without looking at the subject first...so you can "feel" every countour, every edge. In this stage it doesn't matter what it looks like. Focus on the pressure and line work....just practice and go from there..



</answer>

<answer from>



pureness...
Member since: 10 January 2007
Total points: 1328 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



Very good drawings! If you have taken Art as a GCSE subject, that will be a bonus as you will learn many skills. as you draw more and have learnt the skills you will become confident. This will start to show in your drawings!

Try taking a sketch book with you where ever you go, and make sketches of anything you see. This way, you will be more familiar with different angles, shadows, colours, patterns etc.



Maybe using different media to help you sketch and find out which one you feel most comfortable using. Maybe Charcoal or chalk might be beneficial. Also, try drawing on different materials; black card for instance, along with white chalk to give certain effects.

I would say your sketches are brilliant!  maybe you could add some shading/tone to your work to really bring your sketches to life.

Happy Drawing!



</answer>

<answer from>



Hope for the Best
Member since: 07 July 2006
Total points: 6070 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



The first sketch shows you have good observation skills and i think, with practice, your drawing ability will improve. If you look at your subject as a 3 dimensional object and understand how parts of the body relate to one another considering their proportions and what is happening under the skin i.e. muscle/fat/bone structure, then your images will look more life like. As with most things, practice makes perfect and it does take time to develop good skills and create a style of drawing that you're comfortable with. If you want to concentrate on the human body, you could go to a museum like The British Museum in London and draw from the large collection of sculptures they have there.



</answer>

<answer>



polymerclayinkeswick's  site really is awesome.  Here it  is in clickable form:

http://www.drawspace.com

I like Elfwood.com's fantasy art resource project.  While obviously they have a preferred subject, they cover such a wide range of techniques subjects and media you can apply it to anything:

http://www.elfwood.com/farp

Professor Ralph Larmann at Evansville University in the MidWest put up two pages about drawing in the 90's.  They are still top-notch.



The Figure Drawing Lab covers some basics of markmaking and the basics of anatomy.  The Art Studio Chalkboard covers perspective, shading, and a little about painting:

http://www.drawinglab.evansville.edu

http://studiochalkboard.evansville.edu/

Hope  they help.



</answer>

<answer>



It is a crime if it is done on public or private property which does not belong to the "artist".
The content may well have artistic merit but that does not mean the "artist" has the right to deface property which does not belong to them.

Doris
Member since: 18 June 2008
Total points: 466 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



It's both.



Graffiti is vandalism because you're painting a design on something that you don't own, and somebody else paid for.  I mean, I'd be pretty pissed if I woke up one morning and somebody painted a big wildlife scene on my garage door complete with elk and pheasants.  I hate that kind of art!
If you work in "graffiti style" and you own the property then more power to you!
If the art itself is graffiti as vandalism as conceptual art - then do what you want as long and you're willing to take the risk.



</answer>

<question from>



William H
Member since: 04 August 2008
Total points: 96 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



How can I get a photograph of steam locomotive  rebuilt Patriot 4-6-0 No 45509 The Derbyshire Yeomonry



This steam locomotive was probable rebuilt around 1946 -47



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



http://www.lms-patriot.org.uk/photos.htm...
top phot on that page is of 45509 in its unrebuilt form. This loco was never rebuilt with the Stanier 2A boiler and remained in this form until withdrawn. If by 'rebuilt' you are referring to the class's nominal status as rebuilds of scrapped 'Claughtons', this was never more than an accountant's paper excercise. Little or no material from the Claughtons would have been incorporated into 45509. The first two Patriots used wheel centres from scrapped Claughtons: these are indentifiable by their noticeably larger bosses.



</answer>

<question>



Final Piece please help much appreciated?  ?



Art project, On Mods need a great final piece welcome to ideas 
needed urgently gotta be finished buy august 26th please help me



</question>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



The first thing that comes to my mind is a graphic-style piece, based around the kind of designs the previous poster mentions (The Who/Quadrophenia). You could look up 60's/ mod design on the internet and see if anything catches your eye. There are a variety of sources you could use for inspiration. If you're interested in music you could look at album covers of the period. If you're interested in fashion you could even extend the project to include textiles or something. Don't worry too much about sticking rigidly to the 'mods' theme, as the most important thing they'll be looking for is that you've used your creativity and really enjoyed working on the project. In my experience the most important thing is to show the development of your ideas.  Best of luck :)



Source(s):



1st class aesthetics degree ;P



</answer>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



If you can try to see the Who's film Quadrophenia.Mods were not necessarily better off than Rockers,they spent their money on clothes rather than bikes.Mod clothes were no more expensive than bike leathers, while scooters were cheaper than a good bike.I came from a rocker area but wore Mod clothes and rode a motor bike.



Source(s):



Lived through the 60s and remembered it. Ex Art student,later teacher in the 60s



</answer>

<question>



Info on Flameworking?



Making glass objects / ornaments using a blow torch and sticks of glass.
Thanks



</question>



<answer from>



derfini
Member since: 25 February 2007
Total points: 20504 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



The proper name for this is Lampworking.  There is an article on Wikipedia which you will find interesting, and many good sites if you google 'lampworked glass' - try www.lampworkers.org  (this may not be the exact address, but it's something similar if not)



</answer>



<answer from>



Jemma G
Member since: 03 January 2007
Total points: 252 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



It would be best to try out some classes if there is any near you. Also there will be some equipment you will need to invest in, another thing you will need to remember is you will have to use compatible glass. If you use different glasses in the same piece there is a chance they will crack and break apart. I myself blow glass and if your interested in glass you should give this a go! It's a fantastic material to use, though blowing needs much more equipment, time and finishing. 
Get some books and contact some artist that use this technique and ask them some questions, I'm sure they would be happy to help. Some may offer you some experience if you did some volunteering for them!



Source(s):



http://www.britishlampwork.co.uk/

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lampworking

http://www.glassworksservices.co.uk/

http://www.nationalglasscentre.co.uk/lam...

http://www.lampworkbeads.co.uk/



</answer>



<answer from>

Member since: 25 April 2006
Total points: 86 (Level 1)

<answer>



This is in the wrong category, but I'll try to help.



I think it's a combination of your imagination and paranoia--to tell you the truth.  While you were at these places, did ANYTHING out of the ordinary happen?  I'm not saying I don't believe in spirits (because I really do), but all you've said is just baseless conjecture.  Have you found any information of the mentioned persons?  If you sensed that a spirit's name is Sarah London, and you uncovered records of her staying there at one point in time--then you could have something supernatural with you.  If not--well, you shouldn't jump the gun and trust everything your imagination conjures up.

There's a theory that excessive electromagnetic fields are responsible for the paranoia and the sense that you're being watched.



http://www.beth-brown.com/paranoia.htm



</answer>

"Comparisons
It's true that everyone has a different threshold of pain. What one person considers not painful at all could be devastating to another. Some people describe the feeling as a "hot scratch". Some have said it was "annoying". Most say it was not nearly as bad as they feared. But there is a little more than pain alone to consider when getting a tattoo. "

Check it out!

One of my friends calls it an adrenaline rush...Getting the tattoo is not the worst part of it though...it is getting one in the wrong spot or getting a design you decided you hate in a year.  Or, getting one on your shoulder and deciding 3 years down the road that your getting married, but you can't wear that strapless wedding dress without having to show your tattoo....so you have to go with the dress you really didn't want...lol (my experience)  I have 10 of them, in different areas all over and I regret every single one of them....they were years ago...and for what?  That is what I ask myself now!  Good luck

<answer>



i've got three tattoos,and if they were that painfull i would'nt of gone back the second and third time. It all depends on where you get them, the more bony ( ankle,hip, spine, shoulder) the more painfull it will be, but after a while the area will go tingly and all you will feel is the tattooing impliment vibrating and a slight nip now and again. You can always ask for the local anaesthetic spray they use for piercings to be used to numb the area.It also depends on how much outlining there is to be done and how much dark colour there is, as outlining uses a thicker needle and the darker the ink, the more painfull it will be.



Source(s):



got three tattoos and 10 piercings

<question>



I want one photo enlarged for a frame as a gift what size is best and from Where do I get the pic its on my PC



</question>



<additional details>



i mean what place is best to get it developed at



</additional details>



<answer from>



A man called Horse
Member since: 08 September 2006
Total points: 818 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Walgreens!



</answer>



<answer from>



Fellside...
Member since: 11 December 2006
Total points: 2383 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



The main thing is:-  Is the picture of sufficient quality to enlarge to the size that you eventually decide?

If it has been taken at low/medium resolution, there's no way it would enlarge.

If the picture is suitable why not print it yourself on a good quality printer, using good photo paper?

Dependent on the subject of the picture, A4 is probably suitable but for a larger picture (A3) you would have to get it printed commercially but the original will have to be fairly large.



</answer>



<answer from>



ZeDav
Member since: 01 January 2007
Total points: 12099 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



There are plenty of places that allow to send your photo on internet and print them for you (Target amongst others), for the list just follow this link:
http://www.google.com/search?q=+printing...

But make sure you send a very big picture otherwise the quality of the print may be disappointing. For example you could use high quality image enlargement before sending them, check this website for that:
http://www.imagiris.com



</answer>



<answer from>



Pusia
Member since: 14 April 2008
Total points: 3765 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



standard quality for printed pictures is 300dpi (dots per inch)

make sure that your picture is at least this whatever the dimensions of it. You can boost the dpi on something like photoshop but only up to about double the original size without affecting the quality

If you print it yourself, you can make adjustments in terms of contrast, tone etc to your taste - all this assumes that you have a photo manipulation programme of some sort and know how to operate it.



</answer>

<question>

Can someone tell me how many cherubs there are in the original painting called the Immaculate Conception of De Soult by  Murillo?  Did they make reproductions of their original paintings  on some kind of board? or panel ? This picture is 4 feet by 4 feet and has 32 cherubs.

This picture is on some kind of Linen cloth? there's a label on back of this picture
2658
La Madonna del Magnificat
Sondro Botticelli
Galleria Uffizi -Firenze
Giacomo Brogi, Fotog
and there is a seal on lower right with Giacomo's name and the letters are there!
& finger prints left behind!

<answer>



I think I could see the La Madonna del Magnificent has encrypted letters A f B in the arm of the Angel on the left, also if you view the picture from this link:  www.artehistoria.jcyl.es/genios/cuadros/...



The right arm of angel on the right looks cleaner than of the angel on the left, this is the reason.

Also for the original painting called the Immaculate Conception of De Soult by Bartholome Esteban Murillo the link is here: http://www.catholictradition.org/Mary/ga...

I can make out that are 31 cherub faces (one shows only top half of its forehead!) in the painting.

Giacomo Brogi (1822 - 1881) was an Italian photographer.

http://www.gruppometa.it/verdi/en/photo/...
Giacomo Brogi was born on 6 April 1822 and died on 29 November 1881. He started working on photography in 1856. He created his first studio in Corso Tintori, in Rome in 1864. He began traveling around Italy and later traveled to the Middle East in 1868 including Palestine, Egypt and Syria. Brogi was associated with the Photographic Society of Italy. He held studios in:
•	Florence. Via Tornabuoni 1. 
•	Napoli. Via Chiatamone 19 bis. 
•	Roma. Via del Corso 419. 
After death, his son Carlo continued his photographic work.



The link for the museum:  http://www.florence-museum.com/

Look like the search still goes on for additional info!



Source(s):



Google Image Search and Wikipedia

<answer from>



pali@yah...
Member since: 24 April 2006
Total points: 8733 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Drawing & Illustration



</answer from>



<answer>



Psychological Meaning: The eagle is a powerful bird that may represent a powerful intellectual or spiritual abilities. For a Christian it may represent John the Evangelist and for an American it may be a symbol of your country.

Mystical Meaning: Early man considered eagles to be messengers from Sun god. In your dreams, it may be a messenger from your unconscious. Mythology often has stories of the eagle and the lion or the eagle and the snake. These stories are dream symbols that represent psychological opposites such as spiritual/animal, male/female, conscious/ unconscious and thought/instinct. Superstition says that to dream of an eagle is an omen for fame and fortune. 
Hidden Meaning of Dreams by author Craig Hamilton-Parker. 
Dream interpretation for Eagle Dream Meaning Symbol is Copyright



Source(s):



website



</answer>

<question>



Adoration of the Magi



what is the painting by Botticelli called "Adoration of the Magi" portraying?  What is it famous for?



</question>



<answer from>



the cats' mother
Member since: 04 June 2008
Total points: 10404 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



The Magi is the name for the Three Wise Men who travelled from the East following the Star of Bethlehem to where the baby Jesus was. The adoration part is them worshiping the Christ child as they knew he was the Messiah.I don't know what you would consider it being
famous for except that it is a very well known Renaissance painting and the painter depicted his patron Cosmo Medici and his family as part of the crowd.



</answer>

<question>



Is graphic design a demanding career?



</question>



<additional details>



by demanding I mean stressful..



</additional details>



<answer from>



EL Block Creative Studios
Member since: 03 June 2008
Total points: 1847 (Level 3)

<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Many people will tell you it's not. There seems to be this common misconception that people in creative professions hang around in brightly-colored offices and play all day. It's definitely a fun line of work, but it is also very challenging, highly competitive, and intensely deadline-driven - alll of which make it stressful at times.

If you are thinking about beginning a career in graphic design and would like to ask more questions, feel free to email me anytime: elblock@email.com.



Source(s):



Owner | EL Block Creative Studios

<question>



The dutch chymist print



i have a engraved copy from the original print. does anybody know if it is valuable?



</question>



<answer from>



Nonnie
Member since: 21 May 2007
Total points: 401 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



I am not that sure but then it might be valuable

<answer from>



suibluec...
Member since: 09 March 2007
Total points: 1179 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



It sounds like an edition was created by a printing company. In the time this was created an edition of engravings from famous art works were ways of making reproductions commercially. Images were not a easily reproduced, in the way we have photography and all the printed newspapers and magazines, with large circulation, today. 
It depends on who the printing company was, and if they are sought after, how many in the edition of prints, the quality, also how popular the image is.
The links below include info on your print. Check the print maker stated on your print. If it is Robert Sayer, as mentioned in the link about this image, he worked in London.(1725-1794)
Another way to check he going price is to search online print auctions for that title, artist, or name.
I hope the links below help.



Source(s):



http://www.lclark.edu/~jhart/undatedAtoL...
Scroll down to see your print.
http://www.ukauction.info/2007/09/07/lon...

http://www.vam.ac.uk/nal/findinginfo/inf...

http://www.bonhams.com/cgi-bin/public.sh...



</answer>

<question>



How do I copyright my photographs?

MendozaT...

</answer from>



<answer>

<answer from>



liam_smi...
Member since: 08 January 2008

</answer from>



<answer>



i don't think you can... you might be able to copyright future photos but the ones that are out there its too late... there may be 1000's of copies out there try searching google for copyrighting images

<answer from>



Tortuga Tom
Member since: 21 June 2006
Total points: 29165 (Level 7)



</answer from>



<answer>



Don't know how it is in the UK, but in the States, copyright is assumed at the time of creation. My D40 has the ability to implant my copyright info. I also keep the original files, in case a question of copyright infringement should arise. You can also imprint your own copyright text onto your images before posting, or use digital watermarking like Digimarc.



</answer>



<answer from>



Amanda H
Member since: 25 January 2008
Total points: 1230 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



Just an FYI, even with copyrighted images, anything you post on the internet can (and likely will) be used by somebody somewhere without your knowledge. Copyrighting hardly stops anyone from using stuff they find on the web (just being realistic here). A better deterrent is to make any photos you post very low resolution and small in pixel dimension before you post them. That way, at least they can not be used for commercial print processes.



</answer>



<answer from>



MacKnive...
Member since: 31 August 2006
Total points: 105 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



In all of Europe and the US, copyright exists the moment you make something original, so you don't need to treat copyright like a patent.

The problem is when someone rips you off and then you have to prove that the copyright was yours and not someone elses. People used to mail themselves a copy of the photo and leave it unopened, but that wouldn't work now as it's too easy to forge the postmark.

Now if you have digital versions of your photos, you can upload them to a special webpage and they fingerprint them and record the time you uploaded. This means that you have an independent record showing when you made it. No one will be able to argue with that! :)

I use a service called http://www.provemycopyright.com because you get 1Gb of space to upload lots of photoes.



Watermarking them would also be a good idea, but people can take the watermark off and it doesn't INDEPENDANTLY show the time that you originally made it.

Hope it helps



Source(s):



http://www.provemycopyright.com



</answer>



<answer from>



chris_ju...
Member since: 24 July 2007
Total points: 7691 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Photography



</answer from>



<answer>



Don't use Google images, other sites like Shutterbug put a transparent layer over the photo so a right click&gt;copy photo just copies a transparent layer. Its not totally foolproof, but it deters the average thief.

Chris



</answer>

<question from>



Tine B

</question from>



<question>



What are the main things you should talk about when annotating?



I'm quite bad at it and it lost me quite a few points last year, would really appreciate any tips



</question>



<answer from>



Star...
Member since: 13 August 2008
Total points: 181 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Well when annotating....

Use allot of adjectives.

Always describe how you feel and associate the emotion with the style and the color. e.g whether painting brings excitement, love, passion, energy, happiness and sadness.

Describe the use of colors, e.g weather they are complimentary colors, natural colors, Neutral colors, secondary colors and primary colors and how they could relate with the mood.

Describe the use of style, weather if its marks, dots, etc.

Describe the tonal qualities, which could be tone, texture,etc

And also describe weather if looks flat or dimensional.

With all these try and associate the feelings and moods.



</answer>

Thank you



That's a lot of help
=]

<answer from>



tigris
Member since: 23 May 2007
Total points: 22436 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Sculpture

<answer>



the top three are pretty good (maybe the woman not so), the rest on that page look poorly worked out, even like drawn by a different person (body posture of characters in general stiff, not natural. 
Generally work on your shading and observation of nature.



I really like your Mr Bean drawing, I don't think the likeness is perfect, but I love the facial expression. You also seem to have taken most trouble with the shading. I think you are not really aware what you see, so you are pretty good in drawing from photographs, but not very good in drawing things from your mind. I would recommend as a next step to practice at least sometimes drawing still lives from real objects, not from photographs. That will teach you to see what's there and not what your brain tells you ought to be there. Pay attention to how the light values of a 3D object translates onto a 2D page and use this learning experience to draw things from your mind. Or draw hands, feet, faces over and over again until you really know how they look like. I think you can be really good if you practice enough and take enough effort with it.



</answer>

<answer>



Hi Ash

Photography is an art that demands having a creative mind, imagination, and spur of the moment.
Sense of color, cropping, and composition is all you need. Its not the amount of pics you take that count but the quality. It is better to take less pictures that have more meaning than to take umpteen that end up in the garbage.

The lens of the camera is your eye. Always try to convey what you were thinking at the time of the shoot. This way the viewer can grasp what you were thinking at that moment in time.



Source(s):



Professional Graphic Artist / Magazine Editor



</answer>



<answer from>



K..x
Member since: 07 March 2008
Total points: 2384 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



Most people are good photographers...they just don't know it.



I'm just about to start my second year in A Level Photography. I had no idea about how to take a good photograph, or that I had an interest in it (obviously I had a slight interest in it in order for me to take it up as a course). After the first year, I have developed a passion for this subject. I turned out to be a good photographer...I uncovered a talent I didn't know I had.



I didn't look at things like a photographer would..but now I have adapted that trait into my photography and how I approach a subject. Doing a course in it will open your eyes a lot more, and generally tell you how to take a good photograph. With good practise and a lot of enthusiasm, you could turn out to be great!



Learning about photography will make you appreciate it a lot more. It's very easy to do, and everyone practises taking photographs all the time. A little bit more knowledge will take you a long way in Photography.

So the answer to your question is yes. You can learn to be a good photographer, but most of the time it is down to natural talents that are hidden. And yes, if you do a course, you will learn a lot more which will overall make you better.

If you would like to know anything else on photography please email me :)



</answer>

Jim M
Member since: 22 August 2007
Total points: 9122 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Photography



</answer from>



<answer>



Learning technique, how to use equipment, and composition is certainly possible. Ideally by taking classes, but also by reading books, studying images, and practice, practice, practice.

Learning to THINK like a photographer?  I'm not so sure that can be taught.

And the ability to SEE photographically?  Well, to a certain extent it comes with practice.



But I think there's also an innate sense of things (I don't know how else to describe it) that you either have or you don't.

Perhaps you'll know you've reached that point when you realize you never go anywhere without a camera, that you see every element of life and space around you as a potential photograph, and that you're driving everyone around you crazy with your compulsive behavior.



Source(s):



I've been doing this for a VERY long time.  And, yes, I drive people around me crazy!



</answer>



<answer from>



dubh_gha...
Member since: 10 July 2008
Total points: 809 (Level 2)

<answer>



I think it's a combination of both. The best photographers are able to see a photo before they even have a camera in their hands - that's just raw talent. They're similar to great painters and other visual artists in that way. The mid-range/good photographers can learn what makes a good image, and then replicate it with success on their own - that's really only a bit of original talent combined with education. I think most professional photographers fit in this category.

<answer>



Yes and no.

Photography is first and foremost an art form. Just like painting, or singing, or playing guitar. It takes some natural talent.



But anybody can learn things that make them a better photographer. There are photography magazines you can buy, literally hundreds of books, and many websites that offer help. And of course you can take photography classes.

But you will almost always find that the best photographers were quite talented before they became serious about their craft. Then through practice, experience and maybe schooling they refined their craft.

Nobody can decide for you if you have the talent and I certainly do not want to discourage you. Photography is fun no matter how serious you are with it and it is all the more enjoyable now with the rise of digital cameras. In the old days you had to shoot lots of film and have lots of images printed, which took time and cost money on top of the camera outfit you had to buy. Now with a good digital camera and a computer you get instant results and can simply delete the bad pictures--but not before you learn from the mistakes.

Good luck, God bless, and happy shooting.



</answer>



<answer from>



Steve B
Member since: 22 July 2008

<answer>



Great stuff! Remember to fix her index finger so it isn't so fat on the end. Line values are good - lots of accuracy in them. The second drawing - just a suggestion - create a little more separation between main subject values and your background. I think some artists just let the background go totally white as it nears the front figure, to indicate a little separation between the two - a kind of dynamic tension results, giving the drawing more impact.

Your work is excellent. Do not take my words as criticism because they are not that. Your work is excellent and you have great accuracy in your line values. I hope you will continue to pursue this ability of yours, even if it may not be your primary livelihood, to my mind, it surely shows the substantial gift you have as an artist.



Source(s):



I had my own photography studio in Portland, Maine back in the late sixties (the middle ages? pre Civil War? Spanish-American War?? kidding, kidding...)

Hope you continue to make contributions of this sort, as you are clearly highly gifted and skilled not just in presentation, but in choice of how you arrange your subject matter.

Take good care now, and thank you for reading my comments. From Chris in South Portland, Maine, U.S.A. I am 65, and I was a perfectionist when I did black and white portraiture. It was relentless, and so was I - ! Have a great summer, now!  - Chris.



</answer>

<question from>



izzziebe...
Member since: 03 May 2008
Total points: 168 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



What sort of lighting should I get?



http://cgi.ebay.co.uk/ws/eBayISAPI.dll?V...

I'm most likely going to invest in this tent and some studio lighting for photographing children. I'm assuming a light tent isn't necessarily the best option for this but for what I want to do it is farrrr more convenient!

I have no idea what sort of lights or how many of them to get to achieve even lighting throughout. I'm on a bit of a budget so just need something that does the job efficiently really. If you have any links or examples that'd be awesome.

Thanks :)



</question>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



light tent for people? - make difussing screens, then place them infrount of the lights

even lighting? -for people? generally one tries to create shadow and depth with uneven lighting, shadow and depth makes a person look more 3d and less flat

get 3 flashes and then study lighting

EDIT: looked at your link, you intend to fit people in that tiny tent?

use grease proof paper and build some screens, they are bigger and you can move then around and change the amount of difussion, if you intend to shoot people get a white,black,gold and silver reflector

a



Source(s):



4 flash set up http://www.flickr.com/photos/hellas2008/...



</answer>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



You should take a look at studiolighting.net and especially listen to their podcasts.

That light tent looks pretty small—better for taking pictures of products. (You put the object inside and your lights outside.)

What you probably want to start with is a strobe (or a flash you can fire off-camera {see http://photography-on-the.net/forum/show... }) and a softbox and a couple of reflector panels.  Once you have a strobe or two, you can start to get creative—diffusers and light modifiers are great for making light interesting.  These can be either things you buy, or things you make yourself.  I was doing a shoot the other day and found one slave strobe was too strong, so I diffused it by covering it with a crumpled white plastic bag.  You can make a strobe more focused by sticking a tube of cardboard on the front. If you want your light to look like daylight, put a flash behind a plant, to get dappled, leafy light, any time you want. Want a reflector umbrella?  Any old umbrella will do, if you have some white or silver paint. Reflector panels? Pieces of big, white foam core...

I was amazed to hear in that podcast how many professional photographers said they bought most of their lighting gear at Home Depot, too.

The main thing is, get creative and don't be afraid to try anything to get a nice effect.



Shoot in RAW mode, so that you can adjust white balance later and be sure to check your pictures on the computer while the model is still there.  Very often I'll find that the exposure is too dark or too bright, or that there is an unattractive shadow around the eyes or nose.  Check for that and re-shoot!



</answer>

<answer from>



Margherita a
Member since: 03 October 2006
Total points: 345 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Dealers by definition ( and by my personal knowledge of quite a few of them...) are not the people to expect to be totally honest. 
They are in business, right? And, generally speaking, their way of thinking is: Too bad for you if you are not aware of the value of what you bring to them. 
Also, be aware that if you live in a small place you stand a much bigger chance of being offered a  lower price than the one you would fetch in a big city.



Stamp marks identify silver.
They are different for every provenance, ( place of origin ), century and maker.
Sometimes they might be hard to read the, though, as they might be worn out by usage.

As auction houses usually provide a free value assessment, I would recommend you try them.

I would not touch Ebay until you have a better idea of what your objects are really worth.

Also, I would not sell them now unless you really need to.

It is probably best to keep them for when silver' prices are not as low as they are now.

Hope this is helpful. Obviously you have a good eye, so keep scouring boot sales and who knows...you might become a dealer too!

Best of luck



</answer>

Debs W

<answer>



Why don't you look at different surfaces and then get material to represent them, cover a small piece of a similar surface with that material and then sew them together like a patch work quilt?
For example....
Table - get some wood, cover it with brown material maybe decorated to look like wood.
Marbe - get a rock, cover it with material that looks like marble.

Or....you could make a textile house - like surfaces made out of material - sew a table, chairs etc.



</answer>

<answer from>



Victorian Gentleman
Member since: 13 July 2008
Total points: 276 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Texture.
This can mean anything and everything. Try multi layers of paints. Acrylics and PVA glue would be the best way to start, as is quick drying.
Start with something simple, abstractive on a decent sheet of paper.
Don't just do one, do as many as you can. It is remarkably quick and enjoyable. Then you can build up many differing styles of textures with either the paint alone, or mix with different materials. This means again, anything. . sand, shells, straw, bits of cloth. . anything.
Think about the beauty and Aesthetics of the forms and colors of said Works, and you should be well away.
Get stuck in, nothing is easy to begin with.
Let us all know how you fare with this task.



</answer>

<answer from>



katzy14
Member since: 05 June 2008
Total points: 3161 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



Devongod has a great idea there about the seas.



Make a textile painting on wood or hard cardboard.  You could outline in pencil a scene of the sea with the shoreline and then use shiny blues of various hues for the sea. Put some gossimer see-through fabrics in with the blue for effect and more detail. Bend the fabric to make waves and glue it down on the edges. Larger waves at the shore line crashing. For the sand use nubby type of fabric in brown or beige like the sand so you could feel the texture of it if you ran your hands over it.  Use multi-coloured smooth in browns and beiges to form rocks on the shoreline. Fold them so some are rounded and oblong and glue them along the shore.  Use some leather to create a waterlogged branch on the shore and some chenille to make the wings of a stylized bird in flight.  Just some wings and a basic body. You don't have to be a good artist to do this.  This folding of the fabric and placement will create the scene.



Make it more horizontal than vertical for interest and incorporate as many different types of textures as you can find .  I think that is the object of the project.  Get as many swatches as you can at the local textile store.  Have fun with it.



</answer>



<answer from>

Member since: 10 August 2008
Total points: 166 (Level 1)

You could make a quilt- to symbolise the layers people wrap themselves in

a purse, bag, Poncho, scarf, hat or maybe a childrens cuddly toy

<question from>



Bite Me...
Member since: 29 July 2008
Total points: 67 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Typically how many photographs should be in a portfolio?



</question>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



I highly recommend the book "Photo portfolio success," by John Kaplan. It can definitely answer your question and more. I've read the book three times now it's so informative. The book will tell you everything you need to know about choosing your photographs, basic placements of the photographs, ect. Pretty much anything presentation related.

Here's some tips in case you don't purchase the book: 
1.Choose only your absolute best photographs.
2.Categorize them according to subject.
3.Your portfolio should only be one subject. Example: Nature. Then take a variety of photographs consisting of nature.



A portfolio should be minimum of 10 photographs. Most companies want less than 20.

You can buy a Portfolio Binder at any arts store. They range from $10-200+. Before purchasing the binder though, you need to decide what is going into it.



Source(s):



I'm a photography major



</answer>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



15-20. And NO RUBBISH ! Don't be tempted to 
"make up the numbers" with sub-standard images. Better to have one less, rather than 19 good ones and one that detracts from the rest of your work.
www.agphotoshop.com



Source(s):



Portfolios That Sell, Selina Oppenheim, Amphoto Books, 2003



</answer>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



No more than 50, and at least 20.  Anymore than 50 is overwhelming to look at.



</answer>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



Have a look at http://www.redbubble.com/ to get an Idea.



</answer>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



I don't think there is a clear set established for a portfolio.  You should showcase your best pictures and your portfolio could be 10 or a 100 pictures.
What I did is create a 20 page photo book from Costco and placed several pictures some full page, some collages, etc



</answer>

</question from>



<question>



Incorporating technology into art?



i'm doing my higher art design unit and i have chosen to create a shoe for my final piece.



After helpful answers from another question i posted, i think i may create this shoe based on the theme of Pop Art and use the style of artists like Andy Warhol in my design.

I was trying to think of what to incorporate into the shoe based on this and one idea is technology as it is something which is a big part of culture today and has a massive influence worldwide, linking in with the definition of Pop Art.



How could i integrate technology into my shoe design? or what other ideas from Pop Art could i include? thanks, any help appreciated:)



</question>



<additional details>



what do you mean by something personal? don't quite understand



</additional details>



<answer from>



Lost-sou...
Member since: 13 August 2008
Total points: 193 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



When I did graphic art, my lecturer told me one important bit of advice.. Technology always comes last; a great idea comes first. Before you jump in with being pressured for 'modern' themes, pick something more personal; something you're strong with. Surprise the examiners... I did, and got an A-A!



</answer>



<answer from>



Vince M
Member since: 31 May 2006
Total points: 49259 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Drawing & Illustration



Other - Visual Arts



</answer from>



<answer>



Incorporate some low power LED lights into the shoe.  Take it a step farther than those kid's shoes with the flashing "strobe" lights and make an LED screen image on the shoe's open surfaces.  If you can be even more techie, design it so that the image can change, like those LED displays on signs.

You can make, either, images, or scrolling messages. And, by "something personal," you can have these graphics make a statement that is meaningful to YOU.  Something as simple as "Hi mom!" to a statement about man's inhumanity to other creatures.  The point being that, your art says something about you.



Source(s):



Designer, Illustrator and Desktop Publisher for over 30 years



</answer>

<question from>



ellaanno...

<question>



How do you make this kind of ribbon?



Has the ribbon on the wedding invitation in this link been made from a basic satin ribbon, or do you purchase the ribbons in this way?

http://www.picturepalace.co.uk/assets/im...



</question>



<answer from>



Will I am
Member since: 11 August 2008
Total points: 100 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



Is purchased that way, looks like it has been glued like that.



</answer>



<answer from>



Steph C
Member since: 14 August 2008
Total points: 98 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



You can purchase ribbons that way or you can make them.

To make them can be complicated depending on what you do. I think the simplest way which I've used before is just to layer the ribbon to get that tiered effect. Then, staple it down and wrap a small piece of ribbon around it to cover that middle part (with the staple or whatever you choose to use). That should do the trick. Make sure you get ribbon that's soft so it doesn't leave fold marks, etc.



</answer>

<question>



PLEASE ANSWER (OR TRY TO ANSWER) THIS!!!!!!!!!?



I have asked this question twice already, with hardly any responses (3). My my art coursework I need to create a large textiles mural sort of thing which combines aboriginal and urban culture. I really need some ideas, because I have already wasted my time with it, as I had started, found it looked awful and am starting again. The canvas is a large square with bricks printed onto the top right hand corner and the bottom left hand corner. I also have a banksy figure on the bottom corner.
I need any ideas you can give me, ANY IDEAS PLEASE!!!!!



</question>



<answer from>



angela l
Member since: 09 April 2007
Total points: 18817 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Painting



Sculpture



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



There are many beautiful patterns in Aboriginal art see, eg. http://www.aboriginalartstore.com.au/?g=...
Why don't you fill the whole space of your canvas with different patterns but leave a large sprawling area empty at the centre of yur canvas. Here one could put in buildings representing a modern city - e.g. high rise buildings, streets with cars. You could depict this built up area in such a way that the viewer gets the impression that a modern city is  gradually swallowing up land which was once the home of Aboriginees. (Land represented by their traditional patterns). 
I am not sure how the  banksy figure which you have already put in to your mural relates to this idea. But perhaps you can think of something, or else remove it completely.



</answer>

Urban well bricks and mortar, buses and cars.

<answer from>



julian_d...
Member since: 24 April 2006
Total points: 100 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



Experience the beauty and wonder of the talent of these visual artists.



This Visual/Media Arts Showcase includes a virtual re-creation of some of our favourite exhibitions from over the years that were curated from the Saskatchewan Arts Board's permanent collection. Come on  take a look , and enjoy.



The colonization of Canada dramatically changed the lives of First Nations people. Europeans brought with them diseases of small pox, tuberculosis, and influenzas to which Indian people had no immunities. Native people were pushed from their lands and forced to relocate on reserves in order to make way for European settlers. Missionaries came to convert the Indians to their Christian religions. Native ceremonies were prohibited and paraphernalia were confiscated by authorities. The Canadian Government forced Native children to attend residential schools and speak languages that were foreign languages. Colonization and the "Doctrine of Discovery" resulted in similar consequences for indigenous people around the world.



At a later date the development of new technologies and mass media created new problems. Photographs, books and movies portrayed Indians as hostile, savage, childlike and subservient - people who had to be conquered and controlled. Generations of Native people grew up surrounded by stereotypes and negative images of themselves.



Source(s):



http://www.artsboard.sk.ca/showcase/show...



</answer>

<answer>



1) Try using a polarizer to reduce the flare of the sun.
2) Pretty good shot, but looks a little pixelated. Wouldn't blow up well...
3) Too much flash. The highlights are blown out. Try bouncing the flash off something else.
4) Still too much flash. See how the King card is washed out?
5) I like this the best. However, a polarizer would reduce the flare, or you could step to the side slightly and just get the rays coming through the leaves instead of the flare...

Just some constructive thoughts... Hope it is what you're looking for. Anyone can say, "Nice job!" But I think your looking for ways to make it even BETTER...



Keep up the good work and practice, practice, practice!!  ;o)



Source(s):



Pro photographer



</answer>

<answer>



Haha! Nobody "thought of the colour blue"!

The answer is a big NO - whatever Volvo you saw!

The complete colour spectrum is sunlight shining through a prism, see here: http://cache.eb.com/eb/image?id=44791&re...

Other shades are created simply by adding white (all colours) or black (the absence of colour) to a colour or a mix of colours from the spectrum.

Excellent question by the way!



Source(s):



Artist



</answer>

<answer from>



tlw-arti...
Member since: 01 July 2008
Total points: 1804 (Level 3)



Contributing In:



Drawing & Illustration



Painting



</answer from>



<answer>



There are several books out there about using photos as reference, but none specifically about portraits that I know of.  The best advice I can give her is to study up on what to avoid when using photos, how to reduce photographic distortion, and what photos can not be used due to copyright issues.



Three things about doing portraits from photos:

1.  Never use professional photographs.  The photographer holds the copyright  . . . and besides that, copying his work means the artist is not making any artistic or compositional decisions.  Not original!

2.  Use photos taken in natural light.  Flash photos are the pits when it comes to getting a likeness, getting values and shadows right that lend an image form and volume, and they flatten out the image!

3.  Do not copy the photo, but use it as a guide.



A good portrait has the life of both the artist and the sitter in it . . . copying will give you that, a copy in paint!  Make your portrait original and alive . . . once you have a basic sketch and values established, put the photo away until you are close to finishing.  and then use it only to fine tune highlights and details. . . .



Source(s):



I am an artist, love to do portraits,and I also use photo references because of a disability that prevents me from on-site work much of the time.



</answer>

<answer>

<question>



Will the photo people develop my negatives?



I got a roll of film developed in another country and instead of cutting the negatives and sealing it in plastic (like they do in the U.S.), they give you the whole roll of negative film. So my question is, if I take it to costco, will they be able to develop the film into prints or not? Thanks



</question>



<answer from>



MendozaT...
Member since: 24 July 2008
Total points: 467 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Yes.  The process is the exactly the same.  Imagine that when they get the new roll they open the canister and process the whole roll at once then they cut it.  It later on you want just one print you can bring the negatives and tell them the exposure number and they will print just that one in whatever size you need.

There is nothing to worry about in this case



</answer>

<question>



For graphic design jobs: does the employer ever look at college transcripts or just your porftolio?



No spam or dumb answers please.



</question>



<answer from>



EL Block Creative Studios
Member since: 03 June 2008
Total points: 1847 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



I have been in the graphic design field for eleven years and have never heard of an employer reviewing an applicant's educational transcripts. Most employers will grant an interview based on your resume, then base their decision to hire you on the interview itself, and of course the quality of your portfolio.



Source(s):



Owner | EL Block Creative Studios



</answer>



<asker's comment>



thanks to the both of you. =)



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



ToniM25
Member since: 25 February 2008
Total points: 379 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



I have known an employer to look at transcripts but your portfolio will make or break you.  You need to make sure you have all different kinds of samples of your work make sure it is very versatile and unique.  Also make sure it is very neat and all your artwork is mounted on black boards.
http://www.graphicdesigns4you.com



</answer>



<answer from>



social_b...
Member since: 16 July 2008
Total points: 502 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



They would be interested in learning where you have learned your art. But the most important thing is your portfolio. For example, you could have gone to one of the best design schools in the country and have someone from community college get the Job if they had a more clean and unique portfolio.



You will want to make sure that you have everything mounted correctly and in a unique way. One of the worst mistakes I made starting out was going out for interviews with a portfolio I made at school. The design firm had seen so many of the same projects it was hard for them to see it as "creative". Make sure it expresses your art work and your design idea's. Even if you have to make up some projects of your own.

Good luck!



Source(s):



Graphic Designer



</answer>

<answer>



Masking tape is great if you don't have popcorn textured ceilings.  I'm no professional painter, but I've found if you buy a good, tapered angled brush and use a steady hand, you can push the paint right up into the crease where the wall and the ceiling meet.  Don't over load the brush with too much paint and you should be fine.  Happy painting!



</answer>



<answer from>



Jeff (weseye) Wesley
Member since: 22 January 2008
Total points: 1513 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



Learn how to cut in and feather edges. Then you don't have to use tape at all (is much cheaper and much faster.) Use a special tapered (45degree angle) China Bristle brush made for this purpose.

I learned how to in about 5 minutes and 1/2 hour later was very good. I could cut in windows, ceilings, floors and walls.

China Bristle brush: WHITE FONG™ ANGLE SASH

http://www.woosterbrush.com/products.asp...

Good luck.

Jeff (weseye) Wesley



</answer>



<answer from>



brainsto...
Member since: 10 April 2006
Total points: 33368 (Level 7)



</answer from>



<answer>



NEVER USE TAPE . It's difficult to get it right andwhen you take it off you can end up with a mess.
When you paint the ceiling overlap slightly on to the wall.
When the ceiling paint is dry start the walls at the top with a one inch brush cutting into the angle.
Do not overload the brush and do not make the first stroke in the angle but about an inch and a half away.
This gives you space to spread the paint into the angle and get a neat edge.
This way do the top two inches of the wall and then proceed as normal.
It takes patience and practice but it is the best way and the professional way.



</answer>



<answer from>

Member since: 07 June 2008
Total points: 1174 (Level 3)

<answer>



Hi Andre.  :))



I think it is a good representation of what you are trying to do with Corel Draw.



I would not take negative criticism personal, as these people don't know what you are trying to do.



Take first steps first... if you are trying to learn the program, I think it is fantastic.  Keep working with it until you get better and better.  Your drawings will get more technical and sophisticated as you go.



The more you work with the program,  the more you will understand the settings and how to make them work for you.  I do Photoshop.  When I first started,  my pictures were ok.  Nothing to scream about, but I did not know what I was doing.



I have been doing photoshop now for about 3 years, and my pictures now are awesome... I get compliments all of the time.  So just stay with it.  Have faith in yourself and your talent.  You could go really far!!!!  And besides that...ART is fun.



Good Luck.  Thanks for reading my post.

<answer>



to remove individual blemishes i would use the cloning tool, this means you can copy the colour from just slightly off the blemish and paste the exact same colour over the blemish to cover it
another way is you can blur it like other comments have said

i think the face in that picture is a bit shiny and would advice u use some blotting paper before taking the image to reduce shine
happy editing

<question>



Cheapest Tshirt printing company



Does anyone know a cheap T shirt printing company? My daughter wants to tranfer photographs onto material for an art project and doing it manually is going to cost about 25.00 really greatfull for any replies. UK only please.



</question>



<answer from>



durulz20...
Member since: 30 March 2006
Total points: 3249 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



No it doesn't cost £25!
I quite often do my own t shirts.
Buy some t shirt transfer paper for your printer (A good brand of paper will be about £5 for 5 sheets - that's £1 a sheet).
Then go and buy a t shirt. Shouldn't cost anymore than a fiver.
Print out your design and then iron-on the transfer.
Total cost is no more than £6 per T shirt!



</answer>

</question from>



<question>



Why is it



that in so many of those old paintings people have that same type of face?

eg:



http://visualarts.qld.gov.au/designyouro...



</question>



<answer from>



AnLoDeah
Member since: 01 February 2006
Total points: 2157 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



I think it has to do with what is considered beautiful in that specific era. Surely, the people didn't  all look the same, but maybe the artist (knowingly or unknowingly) "changed" the subject a little to fit the fashion. You can see that done today too by all those street-artists who do people's portraits. They always come out more glamorous, dramatic and sexy than in real life.



</answer>

thanks! :)

MendozaT...
Member since: 24 July 2008
Total points: 467 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



The Rule of Thirds refers to composition.  Most people take snapshots with the subject right in the center and the Rule of Thirds explains a creative way to move away from this.  Imagine that you have the picture and on top of it there is a 'tick-tack-toe' grid, now place the eyes or the interesting part of the subject in any of the four intersection points.  This will take the subject off center



</answer>

<question>



CONCERT PHOTOS...????



goin 2 concert whats the best way 2 take photos without them cumin out dark?



</question>



<answer from>



Perki88
Member since: 02 November 2007
Total points: 17205 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Photography



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Do not use your flash, it only travels a few feet and will only light the atmosphere and the heads in front of you. The lights on the stage are almost as bright as daylight. Use the largest ISO you can on your camera without getting too much noise. Manual settings are always preferable.. Hold the camera close to your face, not out at arms length, to keep the camera steady when you shoot.

Have fun!



</answer>

Pooky
Member since: 19 June 2006
Total points: 16608 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Cameras



Photography



</answer from>



<answer>



What camera do you have? Is it a point and shoot? or do you have a dSLR?



It's the lens that is more important than the camera when it comes to a low light photography. The stage is brightly lit-but a flash, no matter how powerful, isn't too helpful. It destroys the effects that the light / stage designers intended. So you will need a decent dSLR and a lens with a larger aperture, like this:



http://www.flickr.com/photos/little_pook...

or like this

http://www.flickr.com/photos/little_pook...

But if you have a smaller camera, you'd need to set the ISO high. It might be pretty grainy--and you may have to hold the camera very steadily.



Like this:

http://www.flickr.com/photos/little_pook...

Practice it at home first if you can. Try different settings, too.



</answer>

<question>



What's the difference between a photo taken in a studio and an average photo taken with a digi cam?



</question>



<answer from>



fivetoze
Member since: 24 July 2008
Total points: 661 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



the concise answer is about 11000 DPI.

I have a HND in photography; plus 35yrs experience.

whats the difference? the guy pressing the shutter... a 30 quid lubitol can make images almost as good as a 5000 hassleblad... (my cousin did the tests, for practical photography (he worked at Colab in the 90's)



pro's rarely use the camera flash.. they use lamps and reflectors... 
and they measure the light with a handheld meter... we know all about shadows and the like...



and they know how to handle their cameras...



most Dcams get left on auto, and johnny presses the button, never knowing what is possible...because he's happy with what he's got... why? well, mainly coz he doesnt know any different.



Dcams make life easy; theyre easy to use, and you can see what you got within seconds... no darkroom skills required at all...



but those darkroom skills transfer to PC's quite easily... because we know what were looking at, and what were trying to achieve.



whats the REAL difference? thousands of hours experience behind the lens... and a neg file with over 30`000 images per year... thats the real diffference... skill, ability and an eye.



</answer>

</asker's comment>

<question>



How do you remove shine from peoples faces on camera?



this is for a green screen studio....what kind of powder if any??



</question>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



I would lower the contrast, under expose a bit, but jack up the saturation. If you need to brighten it anymore you can do it with software. This way you lose some of the shine and color but the saturation will makeup for the lost in color.



</answer>

<answer>



Well I am kinda new to photography myself but, maybe I can help. If you are not already using umbrellas and soft-boxes, try using them. If you are using them you can try lowering the power of your lights and using a lower f-stop. You could also try moving your lights back from your subject and shooting a lower f-stop. I hope this helps.



Jerry

<answer>



Soft,diffused light will work better than any make-up.Either use diffusers or bounce the light off a large reflector.Polarisers will reduce shine but only from certain angles and do cut the light down a lot.If you must have strong contrasty light then use a matte foundation powder.

<answer>



A light dusting of translucent Cornsilk will absorb any oils on the skins surface. The nice part is that the translucent will work on all skin shades, all races. Have a box of tissues to apply it individually to each person without spreading germs or leaving lint on the faces (especially beards!). Do not powder over dark clothing without a cape over the clothing.

</question from>



<question>



Does anybody know how to make hands unsweaty?



this is embarrassing but my hands always get sweaty especially around guys, i know thats just cause im nervous but i cant give high fives to anybody because my hands are grossly sweaty, i wash them daily and that doesnt help i used to use hairspray:p ahha but that didnt help much, please somebody tell me how to make my hands unsweaty, i cant hold hands with anybody i like because of this embarrassing thing, ugh i wish there was a way, please tell me every detail possible:D i just want to be normal...



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



use
vinegar apple with salad
use
Cyproterone Acetate Antiestrogens
or
Aluminum Chloride 
or
Iontophoresis
Aluminium chloride (hexahydrate) solution: While aluminium chloride is used in regular antiperspirants, hyperhidrosis sufferers need a much higher concentration to effectively treat the symptoms of the condition. A 15% aluminium chloride solution or higher usually takes about a week of nightly use to stop the sweating and one or two nightly applications per week to maintain the results[citation needed]. An aluminium chloride solution can be very effective, however a minority of patients cannot tolerate the irritation that it can cause. Also, the solution is usually not effective for palmar (hand) and plantar (foot) hyperhidrosis - for which iontophoresis (see below) may yield better results in some circumstances. For the severe cases of palmar and plantar hyperhidrosis there is a low level of success using conservative measures such as Aluminium chloride antiperspirants[citation needed]. 
Botulinum toxin type A (including Botox ): Injections of the botulinum toxin are used to disable the sweat glands.[1] The effects can last from 4-9 months depending on the site of injections. With proper anesthesia the hand and foot injections are almost painless. The procedure when used for underarm sweating has been approved by the U.S. Food and Drug Administration (FDA), and some American insurance companies pay partially for the treatments.[citation needed] 
Oral medication: There are several oral drugs available to treat the condition with varying degrees of success.[citation needed] 
A class of anticholinergic drugs is available that has been shown to reduce hyperhidrosis. Ditropan (generic name: oxybutynin) is one that has shown promise.[2] However, most people cannot tolerate the side effects associated with the drug, which include drowsiness, visual symptoms and dryness in the mouth and in other mucus membranes. A time release version of the drug is also available, called Ditropan XL , with purportedly reduced effectiveness. Robinul (generic name: glycopyrrolate) is another drug used on an off-label basis. The drug seems to be almost as effective as oxybutynin, with similar side-effects such as a dry mouth or dry throat often leading to pain in these areas. Other less effective anticholinergic agents that have been tried include propantheline bromide (Probanthine ) and benztropine (Cogentin ). 
A different class of drugs known as beta-blockers has also been tried, but does not seem to be very effective. 
Antidepressants and anxiolytics (anti-anxiety medications) are more archaic, related to the former, false belief that Primary Hyperhidrosis was related to an anxious personality style.
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperhidros...



</answer>

<answer from>



kaiy2k
Member since: 04 July 2008
Total points: 3628 (Level 4)



Contributing In:



Cameras



Photography



</answer from>



<answer>



DSLRs are great, if you can afford it. If you are a newbie, and are just getting your feet wet, I might suggest a high end point and shoot.



Nikon D40 DSLR - $450-500
Review: http://www.dcresource.com/reviews/nikon/...
Entry level Digital SLR. Great lenses. Shoots RAW format for best post processing image control. Accepts a variety of lenses.

Canon G9 - $450
Review:http://www.steves-digicams.com/2007_revi...
very capable camera, lots of functionality, manual exposure controls, shoots RAW format.

Canon S5 IS $300-400
Review: http://www.dcresource.com/reviews/canon/...
slightly less capable camera, lots of functionality, manual exposure controls, great zoom - 12x optical.

Canon 590 IS -$150
Review: http://www.dcresource.com/reviews/canon/...
Entry level camera. Better manual exposure controls than others in it's class, small and inexpensive.



</answer>

<answer>



Check for the same camera in Ebay to get an idea. Also, check to see if the camera is available on KEH or BH used department. Keep in mind, they are charging more than what they paid for it to make a profit, but you can get a good idea where to start pricing. You can also check Craigs List in a large city to try to find one listed and see what kind of price is being asked.



If all that fails, just base your asking price on what can be purchased in TODAY'S market. If you are asking $500 for it, but a person can buy a new camera that is much better for $400, then you have to base your pricing on that. It is sort of like an older computer. The newer ones get better and better, and cheaper and cheaper. You can never get near what you paid for an older computer.... and that is pretty much the same with a digital camera body.



The lenses are where the long term resell value lies.

steve



</answer>

<question from>



Nathan V
Member since: 21 December 2006
Total points: 81 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



What is the best way to achieve this stamp/ink effect in photoshop CS2?



http://i97.photobucket.com/albums/l232/l...

I need help taking a normal pic and turning somewhat into the picture above. Using the filters don't seem to get the effect.



</question>



<answer from>



Vince M
Member since: 31 May 2006
Total points: 47744 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Drawing & Illustration



Other - Visual Arts



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Several approaches.

Change the color image to greyscale, and then to bitmap.  Play with the "threshold" controls to bring up the portitons you want to remain black,and bring down the areas you want to go white.

Or, from the greyscale mode, you can use the Image/Adjustments and play with the brightness, contrast controls, begining with high brightness and highe contrast.

Or, again, from the greyscale mode, go to filters and find the Posterize filter and play with that.



Source(s):



Designer, Illustrator and Desktop Publisher for over 30 years



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Second method works best. Thanks for other strategies.



</asker's comment>

<question from>



spinkway...
Member since: 25 April 2006
Total points: 26 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



DIY Andy Warhol pic design...advice pls?



Hi, I am interested in making my own Andy Warhol (Pop art) type pictures using my own photos / prints.
Any advice, good wesites? I have Paintshop Pro.
Thanks



</question>



<answer from>



jplatt39
Member since: 26 June 2007
Total points: 15058 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Drawing & Illustration



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



The best advice is you don't need any special programs or expertise, except design, to make that sort of picture, and if you want to learn how to make them well, if you do enough of them you will learn to.

Scan a picture into your computer, open it with any paint program, and make the changes necessary to have it look like what you want it to look like.

While Warhol was using modern technology to make his pictures, he certainly was not cutting edge.  When he began incorporating photographs into his pictures Nam June Paik was already cutting up machines and reassembling them into beautiful electronic sculptures which aren't yet appreciated, and among others the cartoonist Philippe Druillet was using computer graphics in his Lone Sloane.

When Warhol started using those photos he would photograph newspaper or other reproduced images, blow the prints up and use them as the basis for silk screens, print them, then paint over them or otherwise manipulate the image paying as careful attention to the mechanical equivalent of pixilation as he did to what he was creating an image of.  This tied in with his books, which he recorded then deliberately hired imperfect typists to transcribe and his early movies which covered such subjects as 24 unedited hours of the Empire State Building or real time footage of the poet John Giorno sleeping.

His work was as much about the LOSS of information as it was about information or his putative subjects.  Interview magazine, in its early years, was not subjected to conventional editing by deliberate policy (and stopped being interesting to some of us when  it was introduced).



His later work is of his famous friends, and usually started from pictures he took himself.  There is no question that he was an excellent photographer when he wanted to be, but his point was still to subject these pictures to the same -- homogenizing -- treatment then introduce design elements which pointed up what was missing as much as they did what was there (such as the unrealistic colors).

Paintshop Pro is as good a program to work on as any, but again, I assume you will want to include photographs.  Even if your pictures are on computer, print them out (preferably in black and white) and scan them in again.  Then use those as the basis for your imagery.



Source(s):



ArtNews 1969-present
Inteview Magazine 1972-1982

Art History 102



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Thanks



</asker's comment>

<question>



How can I make these manga eyes look downwards and upwards?



http://i251.photobucket.com/albums/gg295...



Yeah, its a quick drawing. Please give direct advise, not give me a link to a website which *might* help me.

I'd like to keep the eyes the style they are. Any help greatly appreciated :)



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



Move the pupils to the top right corner to look up and either move the pupil to look down or change the light tint in them. That usually works effectively depending on the eye style.

I draw manga a lot!
