<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



there is a scale which varies from manufacturers, but generally goes from 9H (H=hardness) to 9B (B=blackness), which refers to the properties of the graphite itself.  A hard pencil gives you a hard line which smudges little and will sharpen to a strong point, whilst moving from grade B through 9B offer you varying grades of blackness or softness, meaning the  depth of tone you will achieve, and the "smudginess" of the mark. There is also an"F" grade (Finepoint) that sits in the middle  of these.

Personally i prefer not to mix charcoal and graphite in the same image as the graphite contains waxes progressively in the "B" range, and charcoal often doesn't sit well with this. If you were to use both together i would suggest using charcoal first, then the pencil over the top for this reason.

Wiki gives a good chart of the pencil grades, and i will put the link below :-)



Source(s):



http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pencil



</answer>



<asker's comment>

</asker's comment>

angela l

Contributing In:

<answer>



First and foremost Picasso: The idea of producing variations on a work from the past was probably inspired by Picasso who reinterpreted works by Grünewald, Delacroix, Manet, Gauguin and Velazquez himself. Bacon said, ‘Picasso is the reason why I paint. He is the father figure, who gave me the wish to paint………. Picasso was the first person to produce figurative paintings which overturned the rules of appearance; he suggested appearance without using the usual codes, without respecting the representational truth of form, but using a breath of irrationality instead, to make representation stronger and more direct; so that form could pass directly from the eye to the stomach without going through the brain.’ 
also Christian altarpieces. Bacon’s use of areligious format for a grotesque subject  probably inspired by the expressionist paintings of Max Beckmann http://www.artyfactory.com/art_appreciat...



</answer>

<question>



When you get someting professionaly framed what exactly do they do ,also roughly how much will it cost for?



framed degree



</question>



<answer from>



DCReed
Member since: 28 January 2008
Total points: 2881 (Level 4)



Contributing In:



Photography



</answer from>



<answer>



Professional framing costs a boatload.  They put the picture on a mat, put a backing behind it, frame it, and add glass.

For a 20" x 30" print, you can expect $300-$500.  For smaller, "standard" sizes like 5" x 7" and 8" x 10", I just buy frames and mats myself and do it.



</answer>



<answer from>



Mark J
Member since: 28 June 2006
Total points: 11353 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



its difficult to say
it depends on what frame you want
what mounting (if any) you want
what type of glass.

The best thing to do is to take it to several framers and see what they suggest.

there is nothing magical or mysterious about framing, you can  do it yourself quite satisfactorily.. providing you can find a frame. the frame is often the fiddly thing. but I'm guessing your degree will be no more than say A4 so it should be possible to get a simple off the shelf frame from virtually anywhere including IKEA



</answer>



<answer from>



rockstar...
Member since: 04 June 2007
Total points: 259 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Get a Micheal's coupon from the sunday paper and you can get 50%off your order.



</answer>



<answer from>



hushcolo...
Member since: 29 June 2007
Total points: 4387 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



Hi,

I guess that prices will vary alot and since I'm portuguese, I'm not aware of the prices practiced in USA or other countries.
Now, the framer will have to work taking into consideration the customer's budget and not only what should be done.
Of course that it's his/her job to advise the customer.
Nonetheless, supposing that money isn't an issue, the materials used in the framing process will be archival quality i.e. the mount and mat (if used) will be 100% rag thus acid free (mind that a mat can be acid free and not be archival quality since it may have been buffed with calcium carbonate but may become acid in the future).
The use of any glue will be done using an acid free glue such as weath starch and hinges will be used on paper works.
The materials will be reversible.
For some more info you may visit frametek.com

Kind regards,

Joséhttp://www.hushcolours.com



</answer>



<answer from>

<question from>



lacytigh...
Member since: 13 September 2008
Total points: 219 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



How do you transfer an image from thick magazine paper to reasonably thick cartridge paper?



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



It depends on the type of ink used for the magazine.Turps and soap in equal parts works on some inks but the result is a bit faint on others.You mix the two and stir well to make an emulsion,paint it on,leave it a few seconds ,then place it face down on your cartridge and rub the back with a spoon.If you need to soak it for quite a while use another sheet of paper to protect the original
You may need to experiment with different thinners if this doesn't work too well.Try adding a bit of meths as well.
There are proprietary chemicals but they are not always easy to get in Art shops.



</answer>

<question from>



Electro Star
Member since: 07 August 2008
Total points: 659 (Level 2)



</question from>



<question>



What was the Jim Lambie installation that was nominated for a Turner Prize in 2005?



I keep reading that it was called Mental Oyster, but ive also read articles that describe the installation containing over-sized bird sculptures which was The Kinks. So which one was it??



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



"The [Turner]Prize is awarded each year to : 'a British artist under fifty for an outstanding exhibition or other presentation of their work in the twelve months preceding'.  "
http://www.tate.org.uk/britain/turnerpri...

So  the answer to your question is both of these installations, Mental Oyster and The Kinks, as "Jim Lambie has been nominated for his exhibitions at Anton Kern Gallery, New York, and Sadie Coles HQ, London."
http://www.tate.org.uk/britain/turnerpri...

Both of the Installations were shown at various galleries, gaining Lambie his nomination, in the twelve months preceding the 2005 Turner prize. The prize recognises all works shown by an artist during this twelve month period and is awarded for an outstanding contribution in this period of time.



Source(s):



http://www.tate.org.uk/britain/turnerpri...
http://www.tate.org.uk/britain/turnerpri...



</answer>

<question>



Should you cover the red window on the back of a Kodak Brownie 127 camera?

</question>



<answer from>



bluespee...
Member since: 25 September 2007
Total points: 1721 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



Well if the window is letting light in to the film itself then I would definitely blank it off... Have you taken a test spool to make sure?

If you do blank the window off I guess you'll need to work out how many turns for each exposure, but if there is no audible click for each one then you stand the risk of overlap.

Used to have a brownie years ago...



Now what ever happened to it?......

<answer from>



brian.pa...
Member since: 21 March 2008
Total points: 5284 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



No it's there for you to see the numbers when you wind the film on.
The film is fastened to a backing sheet of paper which has a light proof black layer inside.
Wind the film on 'til you see a set of spots and then the number 1,shoot then wind on straight away so you do not double expose the film.



Source(s):



Trained on roll film cameras in the 60s.



</answer>

<answer>

<answer>



Mac or PC, be sure it has at least 4GB of RAM, a 17 inch screen, the largest hard drive you can get on it, at least a 500 GB external drive and a computer that has an ExpressCard/34 or 54 port so you can copy image files from your CF cards at 2.5 Gbit/s (PCI Express mode).  In the 480 Mbit/s (USB mode) large cards can take a long time to copy to your computer.

I have been using HP's since I sold my Mac's (Photoshop CS2 ran faster on PC's, but Adobe promised that CS3 would run as fast in either Mac or PC platforms).

I do a lot of A/V presentations and interfaces at most meeting rooms are interfaced for PC's (and use standard Microsoft AV programs).

If you do buy a Mac, buy one that is dark gray.  White notebooks get real dirty, real fast.  Not something you want to use in front of your clients.

A Mac configured for a photographer will be about



$3700.

http://store.apple.com/us/configure/MB16...

proFotog

<answer from>



Gwyn of Graphite Land
Member since: 27 April 2007

<answer>



No. The Olympus is not a good camera for the long term. On paper, the specs look good - but now go get lenses for it. Very different. Poor selection. Pricey and not very good. Eventually you will want to upgrade the camera body, but ... but Olympus has very little to offer.

x2 crop factor is great for some wildlife and lousy for landscapes. Absolutely horrible for landscapes. Do you love the wide angle landscape photo? If so, you will never see one from this camera.



The top end lenses for Olympus are okay, but ... but so are the top end lenses from Canon and Nikon. Canon and Nikon have a much better selection, and their bargain lenses are really bargains!



I had the Olympus 510 for years. I hated it so much!



Source(s):



Former Olympus owner and now owner of Canon 40D.



</answer>

<question from>



Achtung Barry
Member since: 28 November 2007
Total points: 68 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Could you describe your work flow from when you take a RAW shot right through to printing?



I am especially interested in seeing what adjustments you make in the raw converter and those you leave to photoshop.

I have started shooting in raw and am particularly interested in this aspect.



</question>



<answer from>



andy w
Member since: 07 September 2006
Total points: 561 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



You will get as many answers to this as there are photographers looking.
It depends on so many different factors such as type of shot, end client, conditions shot under, whether for print/web etc etc.

Basic colour/exposure correction should be done in RAW.
Depending on the program you are using then cataloguing/exif/iptc done in RAW

Everything else can be done in PS on a copy of the original file.

Try reading this - I haven't, it is just one thing out of a RAW workflow search.

http://www.zuberphotographics.com/conten...



</answer>



<answer from>



fhotoace
Member since: 22 January 2007
Total points: 134212 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Cameras



Photography



</answer from>



<answer>



I shoot RAW + JPEG Basic

I use Adobe Bridge to append all the images XMP files with my copyright notice, contact information, client or subject information and key words.

I edit (sort) all JPEG the images from the shoot using my file manager and pick the best of the best and place them in a new file folder.

Then I pick the matching RAW files and process only them, saving them as high quality JPEG or TIFF files as the client chooses.  JPEG images with the sRGB colour space are used to make photographic prints and TIFF with the Adobe RGB colour space when the images are going to be published

Then ALL the image files are saved to a backup DVD (or two)



Source(s):



proFotog



</answer>



<answer from>



screwdri...
Member since: 24 July 2007
Total points: 8353 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



First I import the files off the card using Bridge, this tags my pictures with copyright and contact details automatically, gives them a meaningful name and simultaneously copies to one of my back up hard drives.

Using bridge I use a 'traffic light' system for grading the images, red means 'no hoper', yellow is a 'maybe' and green is a definite keeper.

I then view all the green files, any which can be grouped together because the lighting and exposure are all the same (all interior shots for instance) are selected in Bridge and opened in the Raw Converter. Here I settle colour balance, any exposure tweaks, contrast and saturation. I also apply a pre sharpening using low levels of USM. Here is where I would convert to B&W if that's called for by the client.



I try to do all 'global' adjustments in the Raw converter as it is totally none destructive, I use Photoshop when I want to apply changes to selections, cropping and any pictures that need individual attention (Cloning, Dodge and Burn etc.).

I have written a number of Actions that do such things as resizing for web or for various print sizes, converting to Tiff or Jpeg, applying a signature etc.. The beauty of having them as Actions is you can apply any or all of the Actions using batch processing which speeds things up enormously, this file is sent to the client usually via e-mail or ftp and has low res thumbnails of all the 'green' images in it. Individual attention is then given to the images that the client has responded to.

The actual output depends on the client, a web designer will only need fairly low res jpegs which can be e-mailed, publishers need high res Tiffs or Jpeg files often CMYK using ftp or on a memory card and galleries will want finished prints, I can only do up to A3+ any larger are sent as 16bit Tiffs to a local printer usually on a memory card in person and I pick them up a couple of days later.

Chris



</answer>

<question>



What is cubism? :P  x  Art h.w  x ASAP :D?

<answer from>

<answer>

Pablo Picasso

other artists were involved but these two were the main men.

<answer from>

<answer>

</answer>



<answer from>



anarchya...
Member since: 19 July 2007
Total points: 195 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



Picasso, and Mondrian are to reasonably different cubist painters. It's an early 20th century movement (not sure of exact dates) based around exploring new ways of portraying three dimensional objects on a two dimensional surface- picasso often paints the same object from more than one angle. Mondrian shows elements of later cubism in which depth is removed in favour of using the canvas plain, using the painting as an object in itself rather than a representation with an illusion of depth. Many cubist painters started to work with purely geometrical shapes and the idea of the grid. They started to use shape and colour to portray ideas, it was the first really abstract art. The cubist ideas went on to shape the manifestos of the abstract expressionists in the second half of the century. 
That's about all I can run off right now, you'd probably do well on google or even wikipedia ( as long as you're not expected to cite anything!)



</answer>

<question>



Five main ingredients to film developer?



</question>



<answer from>



brian.pa...
Member since: 21 March 2008
Total points: 5284 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



I'll give you the general types of ingredients.
Developing agent
Preservative
Accelerator
Restrainer
Acid
and of course water.



Source(s):



Ansel Adams' book The Negative.



</answer>



<answer from>



mightasw...
Member since: 23 November 2006
Total points: 5329 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



For black and white photography, the developer is typically a mixture of Metol, Phenidone or Dimezone and hydroquinone. These are made up in aqueous solution with a suitable alkaline agent such as sodium carbonate, borax, etc. to create the appropriately high pH and with sodium sulfite to delay oxidation of the developing agents by atmospheric oxygen. Hydroquinone is superadditive with metol, meaning that it acts to "recharge" the metol after it has been oxidised in the process of reducing silver in the emulsion. Sulfite in a developer not only acts to prevent aerial oxidation of the developing agents in solution, it also facilitates the regeneration of metol by hydroquinone (reducing compensation and adjacency effects) and in high enough concentrations acts as a silver halide solvent.

Most developers also contain small amounts of potassium bromide to modify and restrain the action of the developer to suppress chemical fogging. Developers for high contrast work have higher concentrations of hydroquinone and lower concentrations of metol and tend to use strong alkalis such as sodium hydroxide to push the pH up to around pH 11 to 12.



Source(s):



http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photographi...



</answer>

<question from>



curlgirl
Member since: 02 October 2006
Total points: 120 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Does anyone know how i can calibrate my monitor's colour manually?



i have discovered that when i print photographs they come out darker and slightly different in colour than on my computer screen. I can't afford to but a fancy device right now to calibrate my monitor but wondered if anyone has any ideas as to how i could alter things manually so i can get better prints??? :)



</question>



<additional details>



I should mention that i don't appear to have aquired adobe gamma, and can't seem to get it from anywhere - i heard that it no longer loads with cs3.



</additional details>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



Well it depends if you have any knobs on the monitor to calibrate it like colour temperature and contrast and brilliance and a few more.
If you have then look at that print you did and fiddle around till the screen looks like the print.

If you haven't any adjustments then alter the angle of the monitor until you are looking at it and seeing the same result as your print. By rights if you draw a line  from your eyes to  the middle of the monitor there should be 90 degrees between the flat of the  screen and that line.
If not then there is something out in the calibration or the monitor is on its way out if it's old.  There are a few places  on the web that sells refurb monitors with adjustments and theyre not expensive.



Source(s):



personal experience



</answer>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



The most basic way to do it is with software like adobe gamma
http://kb.adobe.com/selfservice/viewCont...

it will not do as good a job as hardware calibration devices but it is better than nothing. then you need to check the ICC profile for your printer and make sure its set for the ink and paper you are using.



</answer>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



You can calibrate your monitor manually using adobe gamma, but you will need to have photoshop (or photoshop elements) installed .



This web page explains how to use it:
http://www.pictopia.com/perl/doc?process...

I have used it successfully it wasn't too hard and I have found it to be accurate. One tip.. when you are adjusting the shade to match the tone of the stripy pattern.. I have found it helps if you squint your eyes together to make it slightly blurry. This will help you calibrate it more accurately. Good luck!



</answer>

<category>

Lakely
Member since: 01 December 2006

<answer>



Just say copyright near or on the photo.

I am not absolutely certain, but I do know that a writer is already covered by copyright once they put their words down on paper, and I think it is the same for a photographer.

I was a photographer for some time, and to be honest with you, I never gave the copyright a though... and never had a problem with anyone stealing anything.

My advice is simply to go ahead and mark it copyright, and don't give it another though.

<answer from>



screwdri...
Member since: 24 July 2007
Total points: 8353 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



If you took the picture, you own the copyright. Proving it is another matter, you will have the original file which will include when the picture was taken, but they can be edited. You can register your copyright - for a price, but you own it already. All my pictures have copyright notices, and my name, address and telephone number. I also fill in keywords (for search engines) included in the IPTC part of the EXIF file which is filled in automatically by Photoshop when I import my pictures.

Selling pictures on eBay is a waste of time, much better to submit your images to a good photo library, but they have to be good to sell. Everybody and their Grandmother has a digital camera, so why would they buy yours?

iStockphoto and fotolibra are good and look after their photographers, both offer advice as to what sells and which market to aim your pictures at. My best selling picture is of a steaming cup of coffee against a white background which make it easy for the ad boys to cut out, but it is perfectly exposed, colour balanced and available up to a 20Mb file. Pictures of 'beauty spots' rarely sell as there are thousands of them already, though I have just sold a picture of Whitby here in the UK, much to my surprise. Art pictures are a law unto themselves and if you have that kind of 'eye' then go with that, but just making it B&W does not make it an Art picture.LOL

Don't go for stock photos, go for 'rights managed' if they want to use my pic again for another print run they have to buy it again.

Both the sites above have good contacts with book, magazine and calendar publishers, but the real money is with the Ad men which they also do.

Give it a go, your pictures have to be technically perfect, make it easy for the client to use your image by including such things as cut out masks which can be included in the file and preferably taken with at least a 10Mp camera and nicely lit etc. etc., once you get into the swing of it its not difficult.

Chris



</answer>



<answer from>



Iris R
Member since: 08 October 2007
Total points: 4520 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



It would be advisable to copyright your images. It is not difficult at all and cost $45 . You can submit all images which are unpublished with the same submission. I make contact sheets in Photoshop and save them as jpeg  at 72 dpi and send in a Cd or DVD. The forms can be downloaded or you can call and send away for them.



http://www.copyright.gov/fls/sl7.html
Click on:
Short Form VA



</answer>



<answer from>



andy w
Member since: 07 September 2006
Total points: 575 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Assuming you are in the UK.

Copyright is yours as soon as you press the shutter button (with a couple of exceptions regarding employment etc,) so you are free to do with the pictures what you want.

There is no need to have them registered anywhere for the copyright in the work to exist, doing so would be a waste of money in the vast majority of cases.

If you are posting pictures onto the WWW then there is always the chance they will be stolen and used elsewhere.
To try and limit the chances of that happening then any shots posted should only be low resolution and it would be advisable to put a watermark on them.
Also ensure that your details are embedded in the Exif/IPTC fields. This can be stripped out but it is something that you should in your workflow anyway.

Ensure that when you sell either a print or digital file that the buyer knows what they can use the picture for.

There is no foolproof way of ensuring that your shots will not be used elsewhere but if you take the basic steps to ensure people know they are your pictures then at least it puts off the "casual" copyright thieves.

If you are in the US.
Basically the same applies except that you can "register" the shots but all that means is that should someone use the shot illegally then you would be able to claim more from them.
The copyright again is yours from the time the shutter is pressed.



</answer>

<question>



How do you draw the human figure so that it is in proportion to everything else?



please could you give detail and list your sources please



</question>



<answer from>



anarchya...
Member since: 19 July 2007
Total points: 194 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



I've worked out a basic way of dividing the figure for when I draw people in designs/ storyboards etc. You can divide the body into seven equal parts, based on the length of the head. The torso and legs (measured from the inside) are about equal, three heads each. The knees are half way down the legs and the torso can be further divided into three heads at the chest and the waist (measuring from shoulder to crotch). Because the neck is at an angle the chin appears almost level with the top of the back (which slopes up from the shoulders towards the back of the neck)
the slope of the shoulders should be roughly a third of a head, and the width of the shoulders is around two and a half to three times the width of the head. The arms reach to about half way down the thigh (on the outside) with the wrist level with the hips and the elbow level with the waist. Everyone's different of course but I find this useful for a starting point.



Source(s):



observation



</answer>

thanks



</asker's comment>

<question>



What digital SLR camera settings would you recommend for taking a photograph of a sunset?



</question>



<additional details>



Please explain in the simplest terms, as I don't fully understand how to work my camera yet. Thank you...



</additional details>



<answer from>



fhotoace
Member since: 22 January 2007
Total points: 134212 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Cameras



Photography



</answer from>



<answer>



I just use the reading the light meter gives me.  Meter just to the right or left of the sun.

For the sharpest image stop the lens down two stops from wide open.

If you have a f/2.8 lens, that would be f/8



Source(s):



proFotog



</answer>



<answer from>



injanier
Member since: 23 May 2006
Total points: 30336 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Photography



</answer from>



<answer>



Use your spot or small area metering mode to take a reading of just the sky portion of the scene, and use that. Sometimes a slight underexposure will bring out better color, so I often try another exposure a half-stop down.



</answer>



<answer from>



fivetoze
Member since: 24 July 2008
Total points: 1497 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



F22.. at about 125/250 on 50asa stock.



dcams default for F2.8, which gives a shutter speed of 4000/1 which is fine, but you have no DOF.



the tonal qualities of 50asa have to be seen to be believed.



</answer>



<answer from>



screwdri...
Member since: 24 July 2007
Total points: 8353 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



You can also try setting the exposure as recommended above, dialling those settings in in manual mode and popping the flash up, having a friend in the foreground off to one side. The result will be a perfectly exposed sunset with your friend correctly exposed with the fill in flash in the foreground, the friend has to be within range of the flash.

Just something else to try.

Chris



</answer>

<question from>



iceskate...

</question from>



<question>



Wedding pictures HELP!?



I'm taking pictures for my uncle at his wedding next week. Just all around shots of the ceremony etc. any advice would be greatly appreciated!!



</question>



<answer from>



Jt C
Member since: 13 July 2008
Total points: 6603 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Cameras



Photography



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



It can be a pressure as there is no chance to retake the shots. But if you have experience with other shots you can pull this off. First of all go to the churck or at least call it and find out what their policy is on photographs during the service. In some places its a NO NO . Even if you are allowed to shoot during the service you probably cannot use a flash there so bump the ISO ( maybe as high as 400 or 800)up and if you have VR use it. Some people do use programs like Noise Ninja (from PictureCode.com ) to help with this.



Get a list of the pictures you need after the wedding from the bride and groom and family for before during and after the ceremony



1 day prior to the wedding date
Charge all camera/flash batteries and make sure you have more than enough batteries on hand. 
Format all memory cards. 
Remove dust from camera body imaging sensors. 
Clean lens elements. 
Pack camera bags/cases with all needed gear. 
Review all your information that you have (hopefully written). 
Make sure you have accurate directions (if needed),any written information about the details, group shots, etc. packed in your bag. 
Get a good nights sleep.

Wedding day, 2-5 hours before the ceremony
Eat something, you may not get a chance later. 
Pack all gear into car. Double check to see that batteries and memory cards are there.



Get to the church early and do some scouting. Take some readings and get a rough idea what white balance setting you need.



If you are shooting with a flash for these get the flash off the camera if you can, also you can use diffusers to soften the shadows. Even outdoors flash can be used for fill light so don't forget it there. Gather everyone togehter that will be in any shot right from the start. While they’re all sitting there, shoot the formal bride and groom portraits first. Next shoot the largest groups of people , and then once you’re done with a group (like the grandparents for example), send them off to the reception. For the large groups remember depth of field and set your aperature to give you the depth you need. If you have problems with people blinking when you’re ready to shoot the shot, have everybody close their eyes, and then on the count of three have them all open their eyes and smile. For full body shots think about camera height Standing, Full-Length Portrait: Position your camera on a good tripod at the bride’s waist height Keep your lens straight don’t aim up towards the bride’s face. 7/8 Shots (from the calf or knee up) - Putyour camera at the bride’s chest level and shoot with your lens straight from there. For head and shoulders shot put your camera at the bride’s eye level or slightly above. Be aware of objects behind the people that can look like its growing out of thier heads. Position the people close in the portraits, if you let them stand where they are comfortable they are usually too far apart.
If you can sneak some shots in here from a higher vantage point that can be interesting additons to the portraits



At the reception take lots of candids of everyone you can but remember THE BRIDE IS THE CENTER OF ATTENTION. Oh did I say that too loud ..... Again have a list of shots they want there. Cutting the cake, first dance, throwing the bouquet etc. At the reception your feet are your best zoom. using long zoom lenses can give you a depth of field that is too shallow, flatten features of the subject, put you out of range of your flash and feel impersonal. So use a lens long enough that you are not intrusive but not so zoomed out that you have these problems



Bring lots and lots of memory cards
Bring extra batteries
A good tutorial and list of posible shots to take is here 
http://www.dpchallenge.com/tutorial.php?...

Another good site to read over is here ( please don't get upset by the word amature in the title no insult was intended ) 
http://digital-photography-school.com/bl...

Go to your local library now and read some books out there on wedding photography 
I hope this helps



</answer>

vsweetta...

<answer>



Stay calm, this is a fun thing to do, although it may seem a bit nerve racking. First off look at some wedding magazines check out poses, look online too at wedding photographers, this is a great way to get ideas, make a list of the shots you NEED to get like the kiss, exchanging of rings...and the family shots you need to get like father of bride with wife, grooms family, brides family...ect...(there can be alot of those) but they are rather easy, that will be the bulk of your posed images, and take time with the bride and groom for their pics, this is where you want to get some good candid ones also, make them act like you aren't even there, as for the guests...just random pics of people interacting can always be fun, also pay attention to the dance floor you will get some great shots from that,  and when the majority of the people are eating take that time to take detail pics, people don't usually like images of them with their mouth wide open eating food...ick...but take pics of the decorations details details details and have fun!



Source(s):



I'm a photographer have done many weddings



</answer>

<answer>



1. Get a list of must have shots from the bride
2. Check out the lighting before the ceremony
3.  You will probably have to use a higher ISO during the ceremony
4.  Use a medium tele photo lens ( 70-300 works great)
5. Watch for color casts from stained glass windows
6. Stand where you have a clear field of view( back of the sanctuary during the ceremony)  Front of the sanctuary at the grand entrance( Father walking bride down the aisle)
7.  Figure out how to be 3 places at once!  LOL
8.  Use a flash bracket so you can get flash off to the side of and above the lens
9. If the bride is wearing a white gown- close down 1/2 to one stop ( slightly under expose) to keep the details in the gown and from blowing pout the highlights
10. Most important- have fun!  Weddings are a blast!

http://www.montezucker.com/content.html?...



</answer>

<question>



How to make paper look old...?



without wrinkling it.

I heard that I can use coffee or tea but how do I use it?

Thanks :)



</question>



<answer from>



Feather
Member since: 17 February 2008
Total points: 456 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



using tea is easy! just soak the tea bag in a little hot water then pull it out, squeeze it out a little so it doesn't soak your paper, then rub it on the paper it should discolor it and make it look old. do this before you write on it so the ink doesn't run.
also burn the edges a little, be careful with the fire and don't burn to much!



</answer>



<asker's comment>



thanks :)



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



TYrant
Member since: 15 September 2008
Total points: 153 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



lightly burning the edges makes it look rather aged. Coffee usually ends up ruining your papers unless you are really good at it.



</answer>



<answer from>



Arlee B
Member since: 17 September 2008
Total points: 125 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



Coffee and tea both work well---you can spray the pages, soak them, drip on them, use cups to make rings :} Both are acidic though, so the archival quality is low. Experiment with different types and weights also. 
Nothing will prevent wrinkling after any wet treatment, but if you dry flat or hung on a clothesline and press with an iron, using a presscloth between the paper and the iron, it will help.



</answer>

<question from>

<question>



How can I be successful in photography? ?



Just as a small hobby at first and gradually work my way into making a career out of it.. Anyone out there a professional photographer with tips to give?



</question>



<answer from>



Perfect Myspace Avitar Kat
Member since: 04 February 2008
Total points: 9813 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Photography



</answer from>



<answer>



I found photography school useful

a



</answer>



<answer from>



Edwin
Member since: 08 August 2007
Total points: 31205 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Cameras



Photography



</answer from>



<answer>



If you are really serious about making photography a career then plan on attending photography school. The site http://www.photographyschools.com lists schools by state and discipline.

To get started, I always suggest a nice 100% manual 35mm film camera with the Owner's Manual and a 50mm f1.7 lens. The Pentax K1000 or a Minolta SRT-202 or a Canon FTb would all be good choices. Using a 100% manual camera will help you learn about f-stops and shutter speeds and ISO.

Buy the book "Object & Image: An Introduction To Photography, Third Edition" by George M. Craven and you will be on your way to learning about photography. You will begin to understand composition and light as well as f-stops and shutter speeds and ISO. The book "How To Select & Use Pentax/Minolta/Canon (choose your make) SLR Cameras" by Carl Shipman is also worth having. It is an expansion of the Owner's Manual.

I know most everyone else will tell you to buy a digital camera and just take 1,000's of pictures. IMO this is very poor advice since it does not encourage actually learning anything about photography. A person in here once said that if they took 1,000 pictures and got 1 good one they were happy. You'd get better results, IMO, if you just gave the camera to a chimpanzee.

Photography requires thinking. Thinking requires slowing down. Slowing down allows you to look at a scene from different angles and try different compositions.



"Pictures are not taken, they are made." Ansel Adams

"Best wide-angle lens? Two steps backward. Look for the 'ah-ha' ". Ernst Haas



</answer>



<answer from>



luvnhate...
Member since: 30 May 2007
Total points: 474 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Go to a library check out the photography section. Also google alot. There are so many websites out there to help you.
If you want to someday make photography your career, then buy a DSLR. There are so many brands, (Nikon, Canon, Sony etc..) You'll have to choice which is best for you.
Check out dpreview.com for reviews on cameras. Then go to a local store and play around with the cameras.
Keep in mind, photography is expensive. So first figure out what kind of photography you like to get into...
portrait
commercial
landscape
wildlife
etc..
Depending what you shoot will depend what kind of lens you need to buy.
I think you should buy a digital camera. I do believe starting out with film photography you learn more, but only in the sense if you are developing your own film. It's becoming harder to find the chemicals to develop the film. Plus if you want to develop your own film you'll have to also learn that trade.  If you get a digital camera, in a sense you'll still have to learn how to use the digital darkroom (photoshop) but I find it easier to use.
Lighting is very important when it comes to photography. Then theres composition (rule of thirds). Keep in mind, if you want to make this a career you're going to have to think out of the box to create photos that sell.



</answer>

Arts & Humanities

<question from>



Johnson
Member since: 28 August 2007
Total points: 145 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



What kind of setting should i apply in my Lumix DMC FS3 camera to take pictures in a stage ?



I dont know what mode, shutter speed, ISO and other stuff I should apply on my DMC FS3 camera when I take pictures of dancing on stage where there will a great absense of natural light.



</question>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



A BIG problem. Optical laws mean you need lots of light but to capture movement you need a fast shutter speed or a small aperture all with a fast setting.
Definitely prop the camera on a rest if you open the iris.
Flash will be useless unless you are really close.
Try ISO 400 or greater
f 8
shutter speed 1/60th*
Follow a dancer (Swivel camera on rest) and click.The dancer will be sharp but the rest will not be.
*It seems slow but if you and the dancer are moving together it will work.
Have fun.
ps try shooting any moving stuff with you following it before you do the gig to see what happens.



</answer>

Lessons are posted on the main website and participants upload their images to the forum for judging and critique.  I also try to answer any question asked of my in the forums on specific images.



Our current assignment is on bird photography. http://photography.about.com/b/2008/09/1...



Source(s):



http://photography.about.com

<question from>



Joel
Member since: 21 May 2008
Total points: 82 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Is there a scientific term for the euphoric feeling one can get after viewing beautiful pieces of art?



I often get this feeling after going to the Art Institute of Chicago. It's a "high" feeling, similar to when one smokes weed.



</question>



<answer from>



AK
Member since: 22 August 2008
Total points: 307 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



You might be thinking of the word catharsis. The term was first used by Aristotle to describe a feeling of purification after experiencing an emotional climax watching a tragic play. Today, the term isn't necessarily constricted to describing drama. Scientifically, it was adopted by Freud to describe any emotional release in psychotherapy.

I get the same euphoric feeling walking through the Art Institute (I can't wait for the new modern wing!), and I would most certainly call it cathartic, also awed, inspired, exhilarated, uplifted, compelled... take your pick.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Thanks!



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



Jack O
Member since: 22 May 2007
Total points: 12136 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



Some people get that feeling and more and become overwhelmed when they visit Florence, Italy... don't know the term though...

enmedows

<answer>



There is something called Stendhal syndrome or Stendhal's syndrome which is caused by looking at either specific works or just a myriad of art in a short time.

http://www.artlex.com/  an online art dictionary



</answer>

<question>



Similarities and Differences!?



ArtHomework.....help! 
I need a little help!  Can you name some similarities and differences of the Artists Kieth Haring and Mondrain!!??
thanks! X



</question>



<answer from>



frugal mom
Member since: 20 August 2008
Total points: 95 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



They are both men.



</answer>



<answer from>



brian.pa...
Member since: 21 March 2008
Total points: 5284 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



I had never heard of Keith Haring,while Mondrian is a major 20th century artist,who has had considerable influence on modern painting..Mondrian is one of the founding fathers of abstract painting,while Keith's work seems to be more in the primitive,decorative and Symbolist in character.
Both have superficial decorative 2 dimensional qualities but Mondrian's work appears more geometric and asymmetric in composition.
A side by side comparison of apparently similar works would be interesting if you can source work from both artists.Try to research their attitudes to Religion and see if that offers any leads.



Source(s):



Painter and lecturer for many years.



</answer>



<answer from>



anarchya...
Member since: 19 July 2007
Total points: 195 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



I assume you'd be looking at Mondrian's later work. They're very different so I don't know where to start! Mondrian is a cubist painter, works with geometrical shapes focusing on interaction of form and colour. Keith haring is contemporary and does cartoon-like paintings, generally monochrome with white stick-men doing various things, a bit graffiti-like, there's a range of greetings cards with his stuff on. I suppose similarities would be formal; lack of depth and focus on surface, reduction of subject to simple forms and colours- although you'd argue that Mondrian's work is devoid of subject whereas Keith haring's still has representation in the form of figures and narrative.
Don't know if that's any help!



Source(s):



various art books



</answer>

<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



I have used both Nikon and Canon camera systems since the 1970's.  When Canon dumped their old camera system in 1988, I sold my Canon's and have been only buying Nikon products.  From time to time I do use Canon cameras when a client has a certain camera lens combination they need me to use that is not in my Nikon system or I am shooting in a high risk situation where the camera/lens can be damaged or destroyed ...  I use their cameras .. Nikon, Canon, Pentax, Minolta/Sony ... what ever they have.

By the way .... Canon is the camera company ... a cannon is a military weapon.

<answer>



I like Canon, but don't go by others opinions, go to the camera store, hold each one and play with the controls on each one.  Which one fits your hands better, which one do the controls come more natural for you to use, take a few shots with each one and compare the results, which one looks better to you.  Then get the one that fits your hands, the controls feel natural for you, and the one with the results that you like better- that's the camera for you! Be it  Canon or Nikon- both are excellent cameras!

Note: I tried the cannon- way too loud for me, and they make too much smoke too, and get really hot!  :-)  Just joking about the misspelling- You can make fun of me when I misspell something!

<question>



The best place to find out about colours in photography?



I need to write an essay on secondary colours for my photography course. Does anyone know where i can find this information online?

Thanks



</question>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



http://www.fi.edu/color/

http://www.sasked.gov.sk.ca/docs/physics...



</answer>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



The term secondary colours is used in several ways.
Basic colour theory,
Colour printing,reprographic.
Colour printing,chemical photography,this divides into several sections



pos/neg



neg/pos



- additive process



-subtractive process
Applications digital.-RGB



sRGB



CMYK
I know this is not quite what you asked but it will lead you into most aspects of colour work in Photography today.Knowing what questions to ask is half the battle,you can tailor your research to the specifics of your course.
Most books on Photography include some of these in their introductory section.The Focal Guide to Photography is a very good starting point.



Source(s):



Photographer and lecturer for many years.



</answer>

<answer>



You're probably best off streching your own canvas if you want one that's round  Or buy a pre-made, cardboard-backed canvas and cutting a circle out of it (provided you can find one big enough).  Your work feels much more satisfying when you've done all the work yourself.



Source(s):



Personal experience



</answer>

<answer>



If you only need a small canvas the wooden frames that are used for embroidery are available in most dress making shops,where you may also find a light weight canvas that is suitable.
Canvas can be glued onto plywood with PVA glue,if you can get a circular piece cut.



Source(s):

<answer>



Does it have to be stretched canvas?



The reason I'm asking is because there are other ways of dealing with this. You could, for example, cut out some MDF in a circle, sandpaper it gently and put a primer on it, or you could turn it into a canvas board by soaking your canvas in either rabbin-skin glue or PVA glue and simply stretching over the board and neatly folding it around the back. You will need to make sure your board doesn't warp though, and the best way of doing this would be to attach it onto a square or frame of 2 by 1 pieces, to stabilise it.



Good luck!



Source(s):



I'm a painter.



</answer>

<answer>



Elderberries are in the hedgerows now,using the juice is good and if you use household bleach on a brush the range of colours is great.

<answer>



You could also rust dye-----collect rusty objects, lay them on a piece of paper that has been soaked with vinegar, cover completely with plastic and leave for at least 24 hours, making sure it doesn't dry out.You can also do this with thin fabrics and use them as pages.



</answer>
